            }
            return "blank";

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="7b57d70acc464f6d8f4cfcedaa62149b">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'a88a49de-21e5-4b6d-a7df-7041b74114a3'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d145089518" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Metallic phase molybdenum sulfide (1T-MoS<sub>2</sub>) is considered as an attractive electrode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) owing to its abundant active sites, metallic conductivity and high theoretical capacity. Unfortunately, the thermodynamic unstable characteristic under natural conditions makes 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> difficult to synthesize directly, which greatly hinders its further applications. Herein, an electron modulated and phosphate radical stabilized strategy is employed to construct stable 1T-rich MoS<sub>2</sub> (1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub>). The PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> groups are intercalated into MoS<sub>2</sub> via a simple one-step synthesis process, which enlarges the interlayer spacings and improves the insertion/extraction kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup>. Density functional theory (DFT) calculations and experiments demonstrate that the PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> can give partial electrons to Mo upon PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> intercalation, which triggers the reorganization of Mo 4d orbitals, resulting in a spontaneous phase transition of MoS<sub>2</sub> from 2H to 1T phase, thereby enhancing the electrical conductivity of MoS<sub>2</sub>. The obtained 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> exhibits ultra-fast charged properties (up to 277.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 40&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, discharged/charged within 25&nbsp;s) and excellent cycling performance (up to 498.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 300&nbsp;cycles at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). This work provides a feasible technical solution and analyses the deep mechanisms on tuning of metal sulfide electrodes for advanced SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202303464-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202303464-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Nowadays, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been attracted various attention at energy storage field due to their high energy density, high safety and abundant resources of sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0001_R_d145089510e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0008_R_d145089510e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Sci.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the intrinsic large sodium ion radius (1.06&nbsp;Å) usually leads to severe pulverization and sluggish transport kinetics of electrode materials in SIBs, yielding low reversible capacity and fast capacity fading.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0009_R_d145089510e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chin. Chem. Lett.">9</a>-<a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0011_R_d145089510e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is urgent to find advanced electrode materials. For anode materials of SIBs, the commercial graphite demonstrates an extremely low capacity of 35&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> to store Na<sup>+</sup> and the hard carbon shows limited capacity (200–300&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0005_R_d145089510e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nano Energy">5</a>, <a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0012_R_d145089510e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a>-<a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0014_R_d145089510e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Nano">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which are still unsatisfied to meet the demand of high power and energy density for SIBs. Therefore, seeking novel high-performance anode materials has become one of the frontier topics.</p>
         
         <p>MoS<sub>2</sub> has attracted extensive attention as an ideal anode candidate for SIBs due to its unique layered structure (interlayer spacing: 0.62&nbsp;nm), which permits reversible ion diffusion and delivers high theoretical capacity (670&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0004_R_d145089510e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Battery Energy">4</a>, <a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0015_R_d145089510e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">15</a>, <a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0016_R_d145089510e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Commun.">16</a></sup></span><sup>,]</sup> According to the disparate coordination forms of S and Mo, MoS<sub>2</sub> possesses 2H phase (trigonal prismatic structure) and 1T phase (octahedral coordinated structure).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0017_R_d145089510e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0018_R_d145089510e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> has the highest thermodynamic stability and is easily to be synthesized&nbsp;due to its low formation energy. However, it has a large monolayer bandgap of 1.9&nbsp;eV, delivering semiconducting property. Besides, its tardy diffusion kinetic, continuous S-species dissolving and low reversibility during cycling lead to fast capacity decay and inferior rate performance in SIBs. In contrast, 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> shows a metallic characteristic with high electrical conductivity. Moreover, 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> has larger interlayer spacing, facilitating the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. These advantages make 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> an attractive electrode material for energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0017_R_d145089510e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, the thermodynamic unstable characteristic under natural conditions makes 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> difficult to be synthesized directly, which greatly limits its further applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0019_R_d145089510e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Nano">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Currently, various approaches have been developed to synthesize 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> to improve the sodium storage performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>, such as alkali metal ion intercalation,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0020_R_d145089510e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nano Energy">20</a>-<a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0022_R_d145089510e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Energy">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> atomic interface engineering<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0004_R_d145089510e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Battery Energy">4</a>, <a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0023_R_d145089510e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Nano">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and heteroatom doping.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0022_R_d145089510e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Energy">22</a>, <a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0024_R_d145089510e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Eng. J.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Xia et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0004_R_d145089510e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Battery Energy">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> assembled 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> on monoatomically decentralized Fe─N─C (SA Fe─N─C) supports via atomic-interface engineering. The electron transferred from SA Fe─N─C to 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> through Fe─S bonds, which directly tuned the 2H to 1T phase variation process of MoS<sub>2</sub> during sodiation/desodiation process, resulting in fast Na<sup>+</sup> storage kinetics and excellent electrical conductivity of 1T/2H MoS<sub>2</sub>/SA Fe─N─C. In addition, the phase transition during the nucleation and growth of MoS<sub>2</sub> can be promoted by heteroatom doping. Singh et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0024_R_d145089510e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Eng. J.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> proposed an Al doped MoS<sub>2</sub>@rGO composites strategy to synthesize 1T phase MoS<sub>2</sub>. The interlayer spacings of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets were enlarged by Al doping and a stable 1T phase was formed. Thus, improving the electrochemical performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>. Furthermore, Den et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0025_R_d145089510e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Small">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> reported that PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> intercalation can induce phase transition (2H to 1T) in molybdenum selenide (MoSe<sub>2</sub>) system. Although these approaches have achieved some success, there are still some intrinsic limitations, such as complex synthesis process and difficult reaction control have limited their practical application. Therefore, developing a novel and immensely effective strategy to realize the phase transformation from 2H to 1T still remains a big challenge. Furthermore, the phase transformation mechanism of MoS<sub>2</sub> need to be deeply investigated. In recent years, PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> has been widely used as intercalation agent due to its large ion radius (≈2&nbsp;Å) and high electronegativity. For one thing, PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> could be introduced into the interlayers to enlarge the interlayer spacings of materials, which can improve the migration kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup>. For another, the PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> with high electronegativity can enhance the intrinsic conductivity and boost the electron transfer of materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0026_R_d145089510e626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Small Methods">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Inspired by these results, in this work, a scalable and facile one-step electron modulated and phosphate radical stabilized strategy is employed to construct stable 1T-rich MoS<sub>2</sub> (1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub>). The enlarged interlayers via PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> intercalation are beneficial to the formation and stabilization of 1T-rich MoS<sub>2</sub> as well as the improvement of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction kinetics. The DFT calculations also exhibit that a part of the electrons in PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> is transferred to Mo, which triggers the reorganization of Mo 4d orbitals, resulting in the 2H to 1T phase transition&nbsp;of MoS<sub>2</sub>. Moreover, the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> demonstrates much lower Na<sup>+</sup> migration energy barrier (0.11&nbsp;eV) than pristine 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> (0.75&nbsp;eV). As a result, the stabilized 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> exhibits outstanding sodium storage performance with high discharged&nbsp;capacity (636.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), ultra-fast charged capability (up to 277.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 40&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, discharged/charged within 25&nbsp;s) and excellent cycling properties (up to 498.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 300&nbsp;cycles at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>).</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202303464-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202303464-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303464-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202303464-sec-0030-title">2.1 The Mechanism of Phase Transition</h3>
            
            <p>DFT calculations were first conducted to investigate the phase transition mechanism of MoS<sub>2</sub> after PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> intercalation. Firstly, the possible existing forms of PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> in MoS<sub>2</sub> were investigated (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Among them, PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> intercalation into the MoS<sub>2</sub> interlayer is thermodynamically easier and has the lowest formation energy (−1.62&nbsp;eV). Generally, the 2H phase MoS<sub>2</sub> is more thermodynamically stable than 1T phase, as evidenced by the energy difference between 2H and 1T phase (<i>ΔE = E</i><sub>1T</sub> <i>– E</i><sub>2H</sub> = 0.27&nbsp;eV&nbsp;atom<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Surprisingly, the energy difference between the two phases decreases to 0.11&nbsp;eV&nbsp;atom<sup>−1</sup> after PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> intercalation, indicating that the phase transition becomes thermodynamically easier (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Based on the Bader charge results and charge density difference (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0001">1a</a>), the electron-rich PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> can give partial electrons (0.16 e<sup>−</sup>) to Mo, inducing phase transition from 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> to 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303464-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/83b58a74-4ecf-4ee0-93da-ac8b913b46e3/aenm202303464-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>

                  </semantics></math>/d<sub><i>xy</i></sub> orbitals of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub>, which will destabilize the structure and induce the Mo 4d orbitals reorganization, leading to the phase transformation of MoS<sub>2</sub> from 2H to 1T phase. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0001">1c</a> shows the phase transformation process of MoS<sub>2</sub>. All in all, the electronic state of Mo 4d is the critical point to determine the electrical properties and phases of MoS<sub>2</sub>. Any strategies that could modulate the electronic states of Mo 4d orbitals are supposed to achieve the phase transition of MoS<sub>2</sub>.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303464-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202303464-sec-0040-title">2.2 Synthesis and Characterization of 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub></h3>
            
            <p>Based on the above theoretical analysis, a facile one step electron modulated and phosphate radical stabilized strategy is employed to synthesis hierarchical 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoflowers. The (NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>6</sub>Mo<sub>7</sub>O<sub>24</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O, thiourea and NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub> are applied as Mo, S and PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> sources, respectively. With the intercalation of PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup>, the obtained hierarchical 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> has highly stable 1T phase with inherent metallic conductivity and enlarged interlayer spacings, which are beneficial for the Na<sup>+</sup> storage. As shown in scanning electron microscopy (SEM) (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), both 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> and 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> exhibit hierarchical flower-like structures, which are consisted of cross-linked nanosheets. The hierarchical flower-like structure is beneficial for the electrolyte infiltration and could shorten the Na<sup>+</sup> transport path. High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) image shows obvious expanded lattice fringes of 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> with <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.94&nbsp;nm (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0002">2a</a>), much larger than 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> (0.62&nbsp;nm) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). Large interlayer spacings can improve the transport kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup>. As shown in the Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0002">2b,c</a>, the 2H phase demonstrates some hexagonal lattice areas whereas the 1T phase displays trigonal lattice areas, proving the coexistence of the 1T and 2H phases in 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0023_R_d145089510e1009" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Nano">23</a>, <a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0028_R_d145089510e1009" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Small">28</a>, <a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0029_R_d145089510e1009" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high-angle annular-dark-field (HAADF) image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0002">2d</a>) and corresponding energy-dispersive X-ray (EDX) mapping images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0002">2e</a>) show the uniform distribution of the four elements in the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub>, indicating the successful insertion of PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> into the MoS<sub>2</sub> interlayers.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303464-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f8fc6fb4-d299-4764-a799-aeb5146f7735/aenm202303464-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f8fc6fb4-d299-4764-a799-aeb5146f7735/aenm202303464-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/02b5b554-8e8b-4d4b-bcae-e7d499993d9f/aenm202303464-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f8fc6fb4-d299-4764-a799-aeb5146f7735/aenm202303464-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303464-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303464" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphology and structure of the hierarchical 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoflowers. a–c) HRTEM images of 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub>. The inset image in a) shows the structural model of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheet with an expanded interlayer spacing of 0.94&nbsp;nm by PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> insertion. The inset images in c) show schematics of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> and 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> structures, respectively. d) HAADF image and e) corresponding elemental mapping images of the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub>. f) XRD patterns and g) Raman spectra of the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> and 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub>, respectively. h) Mo 3d XPS spectra of the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> and 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub>. i) P 2p XPS spectra of 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> tested at different Ar<sup>+</sup> etched times.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0002">2f</a> shows the X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> and 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub>. Compared with 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub>, 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> demonstrates a new diffraction peak at 9.4°, indicating the formation of the 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0023_R_d145089510e1094" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Nano">23</a>, <a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0028_R_d145089510e1094" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Small">28</a>, <a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0030_R_d145089510e1094" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Small">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The calculated interlayer spacing of 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> is 0.94&nbsp;nm, which is consistent with the HRTEM result. In addition, the peak intensities of the 1T phase gradually enhance with the increase of the PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> contents. The actual PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> contents in 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> were tested by inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectrometer (ICP-OES) (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). It is shown that the more PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> introduction, the more 1T phase formation (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). The Raman spectra were collected to further explore the phase structures (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0002">2g</a>). For 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub>, apart from the E1 2g and A<sub>1g</sub> peaks at ≈373.0 and 401.1&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, new obvious peaks of <i>J</i><sub>1</sub> (144.3&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>), <i>J</i><sub>2</sub> (236.5&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>), <i>E</i><sub>1g</sub> (279.4&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) and <i>J</i><sub>3</sub> (336.2&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) can be observed, which are typical vibration modes for 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0019_R_d145089510e1153" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Nano">19</a>, <a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0031_R_d145089510e1153" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nat. Commun.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0002">2h</a> shows the Mo 3d X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) spectra of the samples. Compared to the Mo 3d<sub>3/2</sub> (232.5&nbsp;eV) and Mo 3d<sub>5/2</sub> (229.3&nbsp;eV) peaks of pristine 2H phase, the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> exhibits two new peaks at 231.9 and 228.7&nbsp;eV, which can be attributed to 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0019_R_d145089510e1171" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Nano">19</a>, <a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0023_R_d145089510e1171" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Nano">23</a>, <a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0032_R_d145089510e1171" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks of the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> shift to the lower binding energy, indicating that the charge density of the Mo atoms in MoS<sub>2</sub> increases after PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> intercalation, in accordance with the previous theoretical calculation results. In short, 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> has large proportion of 1T phase (54%) due to the PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> intercalation. Similarly, the S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> peaks of 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> exhibit similar downshifts (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, P 2p XPS spectra tested at different Ar<sup>+</sup> etched times were collected ( Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0002">2i</a>). The 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> exhibits a characteristic peak located at 133.6&nbsp;eV before and after Ar<sup>+</sup> etching, corresponding to the P─O bond of PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup>, while no characteristic peak of the Mo─P bond is observed between the 128 and 130&nbsp;eV. The above results verify that the P species are indeed presented as the form of PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> between the interlayers of MoS<sub>2</sub> instead of P doping.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0018_R_d145089510e1217" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">18</a>, <a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0025_R_d145089510e1217" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Small">25</a>, <a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0033_R_d145089510e1217" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Small">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the characteristic peak intensities of PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> are almost unchanged during the Ar<sup>+</sup> etching process (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). These results verify that PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> is successfully intercalated into the MoS<sub>2</sub> bulk phase.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303464-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202303464-sec-0050-title">2.3 Sodium Storage Performance and Dynamics</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performance of 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode with different PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> contents was firstly evaluated (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). The results show that the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode with a P:S molar ratio of 0.02 exhibits the best electrochemical performance. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0003">3a</a> exhibits the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode at 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. During the first discharging process, three main cathodic peaks located at 1.01, 0.76, and 0.24&nbsp;V are observed, respectively. The two peaks at 1.01 and 0.76&nbsp;V are derived from the intercalation reaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and MoS<sub>2</sub> to form Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MoS<sub>2</sub> and the irreversible formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layers, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0034_R_d145089510e1276" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Sci.">34</a>, <a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0035_R_d145089510e1276" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nano Energy">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the peak located at 0.24&nbsp;V corresponds to the conversion reaction of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MoS<sub>2</sub>, accompanied by the formation of Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0036_R_d145089510e1289" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Energy Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the later anodic process, the main wide peak at ≈1.76&nbsp;V is ascribed to the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> and the recovery of the sample.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0004_R_d145089510e1296" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Battery Energy">4</a>, <a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0023_R_d145089510e1296" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Nano">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The CV curves almost coincide in the subsequent charging/discharging processes, indicating the outstanding reversibility of the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303464-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5291eddb-ce8e-40cc-98db-63eacac8d9c4/aenm202303464-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5291eddb-ce8e-40cc-98db-63eacac8d9c4/aenm202303464-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/985eb4ff-f037-4183-8dfc-ba65849bd9f9/aenm202303464-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5291eddb-ce8e-40cc-98db-63eacac8d9c4/aenm202303464-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303464-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303464" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> and pure 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> for sodium storage. a) CV curves at the scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> for the initial five cycles. b) Charged/discharged profiles at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Rate performance at various current densities. d) The comparison of electrochemical performance between 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> and other previous reported MoS<sub>2</sub> based anodes. e) Long-term cycling performance at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. f) CV curves of the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode at various scan rates. g) The capacitive contribution ratios of 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> at different scan rates. h) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The charged/discharged curves of the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0003">3b</a>. The 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> shows a high initial discharged capacity of 636.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, with an initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 86.2%, much higher than pure 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> (75.4%). The 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> also delivers superb rate performance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0003">3c</a>). The reversible capacities of 490.8, 444.4, 385.2, 352.2, and 321.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> can be obtained at 0.1, 1, 5, 10, and 20&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Surprisingly, a high capacity of 277.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is still available at an ultra-high rate of 40&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (discharged/charged within 25&nbsp;s). The power density is as high as 6.4&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>4</sup>&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating ultra-fast-charged property. The electrochemical performance of the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode stands out among the previous reported MoS<sub>2</sub> based anodes for SIBs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0003">3d</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0020_R_d145089510e1387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nano Energy">20</a>, <a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0035_R_d145089510e1387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nano Energy">35</a>-<a href="#aenm202303464-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303464-bib-0042_R_d145089510e1387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cycling stability of the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is further evaluated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0003">3e</a>). An extremely high capacity of 498.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> can still be maintained after 300&nbsp;cycles without significant capacity fading, exhibiting superior cycling reversibility. However, the capacity of pristine 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> quickly declines during the cycling and only reaches 252.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> beyond 300&nbsp;cycles.</p>
            
            <p>To further comprehend the promotion of 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> on Na<sup>+</sup> storage reaction kinetic, the CV curves were collected at different scan rates (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0003">3f</a>). As the scan rate increases, the slightly peak shift can be observed, demonstrating lower polarization during the electrochemical process. The relationship between the currents <i>i</i> and the scan rates <i>v</i> follows the Equation <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information, the <i>b</i>-values for peak1, peak2 and peak3 of the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> anodes are 0.99, 1.01 and 0.99, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a>, Supporting Information), indicating the reaction kinetic is mainly dominated by the capacitive reaction. Moreover, the capacitive contribution for 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> anodes can be further quantified based on the Equation <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0003">3g</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b</a>, Supporting Information, the percentage of capacitive contribution increases with the scan rates and can reach 95% at 1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, much higher than pure 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The high capacitive contribution ratios of 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> would lead to ultrafast reaction kinetics, which make for the ultra-fast rate capability of 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0003">3c</a>).</p>
            
            <p>The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) test were used to understand the reaction kinetics in depth. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient <i>D</i> values can be calculated using the Equation <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information. The GITT profiles for the two samples are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information. Notably, the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> exhibits higher diffusion coefficient than 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> during the redox process, indicating much superior sodium storage kinetics (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0003">3h</a>). The Nyquist plots and fitted charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) values of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> and 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode before and after 50&nbsp;cycles at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> are compared, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Before cycling, the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> anode delivers a much lower <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value (5.0&nbsp;Ω) than the 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode (11.2&nbsp;Ω), indicating much better electrical conductivity. After 50&nbsp;cycles, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode increased by 10.9&nbsp;Ω, while that of 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> only increased by 3.2&nbsp;Ω, still delivering superior electrical conductivity. The above result confirms that the large interlayer spacing introduced by PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> intercalation and high electrical conductivity of 1T phase in 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> provide more active sites, lower sodium ion diffusion barrier and fast electron transfer. It's merited that 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> has improved capacitive contribution and accelerated ion diffusion kinetics, exhibiting vastly improved rate capability.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303464-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202303464-sec-0060-title">2.4 Sodium Storage Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>In order to study the electrochemical reaction mechanism and the phase evolution of 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> anode during the sodiation/desodiation process, ex-situ Raman patterns at different stages were tested (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0004">4a</a>). In the initial discharging process, the characteristic peaks of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> at 380 and 405&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> gradually weaken and finally disappear at 0.5&nbsp;V. In contrast, the three main peaks at 147, 279 and 334&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> of 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> persist at discharged state (0.5&nbsp;V), indicating the structure variation from 2H to 1T phase during the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion. At fully discharged state (0.2&nbsp;V), all the peaks disappear, which can be attributed to the breaking of the Mo─S bonds and the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>S and Mo originated from the conversion reaction. During the charging process, all the peaks gradually reappear. And after 50&nbsp;cycles at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the characteristic peaks of 1T and 2H phases still exist, indicating that the 1T phase exists stably during the cycling (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, at fully charged state (3.0&nbsp;V), the Mo 3d XPS spectra&nbsp;of the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode shows the reformation of the&nbsp;2H and 1T phase MoS<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0004">4b</a>). The P 2p XPS spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0004">4c</a>) shows the characteristic peak at 133.5&nbsp;eV, which is ascribed to the P─O bond of PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup>. Moreover, the characteristic peaks of PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> still exist after 50 cycles at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). This result verifies that PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> exists stably in the material after electrochemical process. The HRTEM images of the discharged state (0.2&nbsp;V) show the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>S and Mo originated from the conversion reaction (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). At fully charged state (3.0&nbsp;V), the HRTEM images show hexagonal lattice regions and trigonal lattice regions, proving the stable coexistence of the 1T, and 2H phases in 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0004">4d</a>). Moreover, the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode delivers obvious expanded lattice fringes (1.08&nbsp;nm) compared to the pristine 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoflowers (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0004">4e,f</a>). The EDS mapping images (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information) show the uniform distribution of the four elements in the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> at the charged state (3.0&nbsp;V). The above results confirm the high electrochemical reversibility and stability of 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> during the electrochemical process.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303464-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a80b3a40-96fc-469c-8e4f-e00711f765a5/aenm202303464-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a80b3a40-96fc-469c-8e4f-e00711f765a5/aenm202303464-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/76583f3a-631d-486a-8dec-ddce2301f233/aenm202303464-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a80b3a40-96fc-469c-8e4f-e00711f765a5/aenm202303464-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303464-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303464" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Charged/discharged curves and ex-situ Raman patterns of 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode at various charged/discharged states. b) Mo 3d XPS spectra before and after charged to 3.0 V. c) P 2p XPS spectrum of the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode at charged state. d–f) HRTEM images of the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode at charged state. g) Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energies and corresponding adsorption structures of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> and 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub>, respectively.&nbsp;h) The total density of states of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> and 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub>. i) Schematic diagram of the migration path of Na<sup>+</sup> in the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> interlayer. j) The diffusion energy barriers of Na<sup>+</sup> in 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> and 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub>, respectively.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>DFT calculations were further performed to gain a deeper insight into the effect of the introduction of 1T phase by PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> intercalation in promoting the sodium storage performance. The optimized Na<sup>+</sup> adsorbed structures of the 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> and 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information. The adsorption energy (<i>ΔE</i><sub>a</sub>) between 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> and Na<sup>+</sup> is −2.66&nbsp;eV, much negative than that of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> (−1.25&nbsp;eV) and 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> (−2.12&nbsp;eV), indicating that PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> intercalation endows more easily electrochemical reaction and improves the electrochemical kinetics (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0004">4g</a>). The total density of states (TDOS) patterns of the 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub>, 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> and 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0004">4h</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information. There is an obvious bandgap (1.2&nbsp;eV) in bulk 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> near the Fermi level but disappears in 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub>. It is worth mentioning&nbsp;that the electron state density is further increased in 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub>, which indicates that PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> intercalation can further enhance the electrical conductivity of MoS<sub>2</sub>. Furthermore, the diffusion behaviors of Na<sup>+</sup> between the interlayers of the 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> and 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> are evaluated, respectively. And the corresponding diffusion paths are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0004">4i</a>. The 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> exhibits a much lower diffusion energy barrier (0.11&nbsp;eV) than 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> (0.75&nbsp;eV). The result verifies that the introduction of 1T phase by PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> intercalation would accelerate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0004">4j</a>).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303464-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202303464-sec-0070-title">2.5 Practical Applications of Sodium Ion Full Cells</h3>
            
            <p>Motivated by the excellent Na<sup>+</sup>-storage performance of the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode, the Na<sup>+</sup> full cells were fabricated by utilizing Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C (NVP/C) as the cathodes and 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> as the anodes to further evaluate the actual application of the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0005">5a</a>). The electrochemical performance of the NVP/C||Na half cells is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information. By comparing the CV curves of the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> and NVP/C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0005">5b</a>), the voltage range of the full cells is set to 1.0-3.7&nbsp;V. At 0.5C, the NVP/C||1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> full cells provide first discharged capacity of 103.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (calculated based on mass loadings of cathodes) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0005">5c</a>), with an high ICE of 99.7%, achieving a considerable energy density of 306.4 Wh⋅kg<sup>−1</sup>. The NVP/C||1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> full cells also exhibit superb rate performance, achieving a discharged capacity of 67.6&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> even at high rate of 10C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0005">5d</a>). Furthermore, the excellent cycling performance can be also achieved. The capacity of 86.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is obtained with the capacity retention of 88% and a high average CE of 99.5% at 1C beyond 100 cycles. Besides, at the high rate of 5C, the high capacity retention of 81% can be obtained after 250 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303464-fig-0005">5e</a>). The superior rate performance and excellent cycling performance of NVP/C||1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> full cells demonstrate its potential applications in advanced energy storage devices.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303464-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f230fbfb-a248-44a5-a62b-dd1060b88da0/aenm202303464-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f230fbfb-a248-44a5-a62b-dd1060b88da0/aenm202303464-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cade9bfe-1c93-4594-9be8-080c5dae246e/aenm202303464-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f230fbfb-a248-44a5-a62b-dd1060b88da0/aenm202303464-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303464-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303464" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of NVP/C||1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> full cells. b) The comparison of CV curves of the 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> and NVP/C electrode at 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. c) Charged/discharged profiles at 0.5&nbsp;C. d) Rate performance from 0.5 to 10&nbsp;C and e) Cycling performance at 1 and 5&nbsp;C for the NVP/C||1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> full cells.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202303464-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202303464-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, an electron modulated and phosphate radical stabilized strategy has been developed to construct stabilized 1T-rich MoS<sub>2</sub> for ultra-fast-charged sodium ion storage. The exploration of the phase transition mechanism shows that the electronic states of Mo 4d are the key to determine the phases and electronic properties of MoS<sub>2</sub>. The part charge of the PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> is transferred to Mo upon PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> intercalation, inducing the spontaneous transformation of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> to 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> and successfully realizing the enhanced electrical conductivity and expanded interlayer spacings of the electrode material. As a consequence, the obtained 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> exhibits ultra-fast-charged property (up to 277.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 40&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, discharged/charged within 25&nbsp;s) and superior cycling stability (up to 498.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 300&nbsp;cycles at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). The ex-situ Raman, XPS and HRTEM results confirm the high reversibility of 1T-P-MoS<sub>2</sub> during the electrochemical process. Furthermore, DFT calculations demonstrate that the introduction of 1T phase by PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> intercalation can enhance the electrical conductivity and accelerate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics of MoS<sub>2</sub>. This handy and propagable electron modulation induced phase-variation engineering and the deep mechanism analysis could provide a feasible technical solution for phase tuning of materials and fabricate a class of metal sulfide electrode materials for advanced energy storage.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
